<div id="review_407601991" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/407601991" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/407601991?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">this is all I know</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      I was wrong to try and free you
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer407601991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1452792139017428164"><i>Is that how he meant it? Before Otto could answer she went on, lowering her eye again, - No, how did he know what he meant. When people tell a truth they do not understand what they mean, they say it by accident, it goes through them and they do not recognize it until someone accuses them of telling the truth, then they try to recover it as their own and it escapes.</i><br/><br/>I want to tell what I mean, what my truth is, without fearing what came out is not what I meant, without hoping what came out sounde</span>
  <span id="freeText1452792139017428164" style="display:none"><i>Is that how he meant it? Before Otto could answer she went on, lowering her eye again, - No, how did he know what he meant. When people tell a truth they do not understand what they mean, they say it by accident, it goes through them and they do not recognize it until someone accuses them of telling the truth, then they try to recover it as their own and it escapes.</i><br /><br />I want to tell what I mean, what my truth is, without fearing what came out is not what I meant, without hoping what came out sounded smarter than what I really meant, without pretending that was what I meant if someone else puts what I said in wider words than my chasm could fit. I can't finish reading my reviews the whole way through to even spell check them. I want to write what I mean and be understood. Is there a way to tell the truth as a bell? I want faces, lives, stories, truths, people being real, other people, places. Touching, not owned, not my life. It is my not alone. I know the world is fake, cold, spent. It doesn't have to be that way. That's what I want. The enough part when I know it doesn't have to be that way. It's not always that way.<br /><br /><br /><i>“-This … these … the art historians and the critics talking about every objects and … everything having its own form and density and … its own character in Flemish paintings, but is that all there is to it? Do you know why everything does? Because they found God everywhere. There was nothing God did not watch over, nothing, and so this … and so in the painting every detail reflects … God’s concern with the most insignificant objects in life, with everything, because God did not relax for an instant then, and neither could the painter then. Do you get the perspective in this? he demanded, thrusting the rumpled reproduction before them. -There isn’t any.”</i><br /><br />I want to tell you that I want this. I think about this all of the time. I write about this all of the time. It's my running into my fire even though I'm going to be wrong, it's going to get me no where. I just want stories to be real. Stories are real. They are everywhere. Some people see the perspective in everything and they can tell that truth.<br /><br /><br /><i>It was the uncircumscribed, unbearable, infinitely extended, indefinitely divisible void where she swam in orgasm, soaring into a vastness away from the heaving indignity of the posture she shared; the world of music so intensely known that nothing exists but the music; it was the world of ecstasy they all approximated by different paths, one world in which temporary residence is prohibited, as the agonies of recall attest: “Love’s dart” that wounds but does not kill; the ill complained of, but prized above every joy and earthly good; “sweet cautery”, the “stolen heart,” the “ravished understanding,” the “rape of love”: in Provencal, conoscenza. Thus Saint Teresa, quadrupedis, “dying of not being able to die”.</i><br /><br />I'll tell you that I don't believe anyone wants to be a painting, a muse, the rowboat or the book of love/heaven/Santa's list.<br /><br /><i>He wants to be Ibsen for just that moment, and dedicate his play to someone who's been kind to him, is that lying? It isn't as bad as people doing work they have no respect for at all. Everybody has that feeling when they look at a work of art and it's right, that sudden familiarity, a sort of... recognition, as though they were creating it themselves, as though it were being created through them while they look at it or listen to it and, it shouldn't be sinful to want to have created beauty?</i><br /><br />This is all I have ever wanted.<br /><br /><i>Tell them, as the composer predicted, there's nothing left but knowledge and evidence, and art's become a sort of tailbone surviving in us from that good prehensile tail we held on with then.</i><br /><br />No, people die. There will be another eye, another feeling and God is not dead. <br /><br />I wanted mirrors. I wanted to see something. When they become real to me and people who are real become more real to me because it is all together in me of things that happened. I would want to read How to Make Friends and Influence People to make friends too. The burned sun spots on the retinas when your dream is allowed like when Stanley is alone on the ship to see hard and arms and fire. When you are alone with the god dreams art and you are painting it yourself. It's when you dream your life away instead of what really happens like romantic encounters that never actually happened. You want this, you expect this. Maybe they don't really want the world. Maybe people don't actually want to pry into anyone's life. It doesn't have to mean that. I want the enough part that's not suspicion. I guess they say that this is a girl thing to do to analyze encounters to death until they don't resemble what transpired. It's playing a song in your head until you can get home to listen to it on repeat for hours. One way is painfully better and the other is not enough and it is the best you've got. It's making your own life. Maybe it's wrong or maybe it's trying to live on separate planes. One isn't always as hard as the other. I liked reading about the people interactions and seeing the two sides, like different places to live that's not always as hard as the real one. Who am I kidding? I never want to get married. <br /><br />I love art. I never gave a shit about what anyone expected to happen once they made the art. I'm copying and copying and not living and living for what could be good enough. I want to tell you that is what art is to me. It's a way to not be suspicious. To capture a lost moment, without being suspicious. I want to recognize and make art so that everything can always be like that. I would copy and not even know I was doing it. I'd repeat their faces and try to find everyone. I don't blame anyone.<br /><br /><i>“Tragedy was foresworn, in ritual denial of the ripe knowledge that we are drawing away from one another, that we share only one thing, share the fear of belonging to another, or to others, or to God; love or money, tender equated in advertising and the world, where only money is currency, and under dead trees and brittle ornaments prehensile hands exchange forgeries of what the heart dare not surrender.”</i><br /><br />I'm awfully tired of me going on about what I wanted to say, what I couldn't say, what I meant to say. Can someone say it for me so I could sing along? There can be another The Recognitions review. I know about trying to live life and trying to find ways that makes it not so hard. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1452792139017428164" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_175711803" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175711803" />
    <a title="David Lentz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz"><img alt="David Lentz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473090881p2/3604115.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175711803?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lentz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lentz" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz">David Lentz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175711803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9398132145303579621">In a habit I sustained in college I make it a practice to underline the most quotable lines of novels I read: The Recognitions has underlines on every page. Gaddis is a major literary talent who hasn't yet even begun to receive the following of which he is worthy. This novel concerns the discoveries, both major and minor, of what is authentic in life: The Recognitions is enlightening, almost beatific, in the way in which it focuses upon the shortcomings and moral lapses of humans in pursuit of t</span>
  <span id="freeText9398132145303579621" style="display:none">In a habit I sustained in college I make it a practice to underline the most quotable lines of novels I read: The Recognitions has underlines on every page. Gaddis is a major literary talent who hasn't yet even begun to receive the following of which he is worthy. This novel concerns the discoveries, both major and minor, of what is authentic in life: The Recognitions is enlightening, almost beatific, in the way in which it focuses upon the shortcomings and moral lapses of humans in pursuit of true art. From the starving painter whose unappreciated genius leads him to forge Flemish masters to a musician whose copied work played upon a great pipe organ brings down a chapel to counterfeitors of money and plagiarists of drama, this of work of Gaddis is the real thing. It is brilliant, witty, original and his command of the language is breathtakingly stunning in its execution. One can see the influence of James Joyce throughout the writing in an experimental style that is breakthrough. It is incredibly inventive and funny and astonishingly intelligent. It's no wonder that The Recognitions went unrecognized for so tragically long -- Gaddis is, without doubt, one of the top half-dozen of American literary novelists of the 20th century ranking with Bellow, Barth, Vonnegut, Hemingway and Faulkner. The writing is work by a fellow of verifiable genius: I strongly recommend that you to discover Gaddis -- he will enrich your life and help you better understand the nature of the personal epiphanies that give meaning to life. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9398132145303579621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1778148876" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1778148876" />
    <a title="Linda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/440212-linda"><img alt="Linda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588731617p2/440212.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1778148876?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda" href="/user/show/440212-linda">Linda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440212-linda?shelf=boxalls-1001-books-before-death">boxalls-1001-books-before-death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440212-linda?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440212-linda?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/440212-linda?shelf=own_scanned-books">own_scanned-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1778148876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer792436856089559230">I've been putting off writing this review because I didn't know how many stars to ultimately rate this novel. After much deliberation, I've settled on 4 stars if only for the simple reason that among the "must-read, difficult, long novels" that this book is usually grouped with, I enjoyed it much more than <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses' title='Ulysses by James Joyce' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ulysses</a> (which I gave 3 stars), yet not quite as much as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6759.Infinite_Jest' title='Infinite Jest by David Foster Wallace' rel='nofollow noopener'>Infinite Jest</a> (which I gave 5 stars). As far as being a difficult read, I found that it also fell between these two novels.<br/><br/><b>Reasons for </b></span>
  <span id="freeText792436856089559230" style="display:none">I've been putting off writing this review because I didn't know how many stars to ultimately rate this novel. After much deliberation, I've settled on 4 stars if only for the simple reason that among the "must-read, difficult, long novels" that this book is usually grouped with, I enjoyed it much more than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a> (which I gave 3 stars), yet not quite as much as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6759.Infinite_Jest" title="Infinite Jest by David Foster Wallace" rel="nofollow noopener">Infinite Jest</a> (which I gave 5 stars). As far as being a difficult read, I found that it also fell between these two novels.<br /><br /><b>Reasons for a 4-5 star rating:</b><br />1. The audio book by Nick Sullivan was absolutely fabulous. I started reading the tree book and got through about 200 pages before I was afraid I would not be able to keep up the pace and I switched to the audio. I'm so glad I did because the narrator brought these many characters to life. I laughed out loud so many times simply by imagining these characters in my mind as I listened. (Ed Feasly, I'm thinking of you first and foremost!) However, even after listening to the audio I would routinely go back to the book and reread scenes to make sure that what I remember I heard was correct, or for the simple fact that the scene was hilarious and I wanted to reread it. I think the audio and text versions of this book nicely complimented each other.<br /><br />2. I thought this novel was going to mainly be about Wyatt and his forgeries in the art world. Beyond that, I didn't really know what the novel was going to entail. What I got in this novel, though, was a cast of many (supposedly 50+) characters, all somehow involved in their own forgeries - art, writing, advertisement, personal self, and so on. The scope of this novel, the many plots that wound, intersected, and rewound was impressive, to say the least. Some of my favorite scenes in this novel were where parties are being thrown and we get to listen in on the multitude of these characters with their various backgrounds converse with each other, while the night continues on and the liquor flows. <br /><br />3. Some of the situations the characters found themselves in were just so completely hilarious, I couldn't believe what I was reading. I was pleasantly surprised by the humor in this book.<br /><br />4. I finished this novel a couple of days ago, and I'm still thinking about the fate of the characters and specific scenes. Some were left in the most ridiculous comedic situations, others were left with pathetic sadness. I suspect I will be thinking about these characters for a long time to come.<br /><br />5. Otto.<br /><br />6. Ed Feasly.<br /><br />7. Subplots involving mustaches, severed limbs, Huki-lau's belt, "Chr-ast", Fuller's suspicions, arm slings, the list goes on.<br /><br /><b>Reasons for a 2-3 star rating:</b><br />1. There is a lot of religious stuff in here that just went right over my head. Even referring to a website that contained annotations of the many references, I still didn't understand. I feel like it would be a full-time research project just to understand all of what was thrown in here.<br /><br />2. There were sometimes pages of narration, letters, or monologues where I had absolutely no idea what what being said. It was like I understood the meaning of the individual words, but I didn't understand them strung together as sentences, let alone paragraphs that bled into pages.<br /><br />3. If it were not for the audio book, I probably would have had no idea who was actually speaking in some of the dialogue. There were times where I wondered, after going back to reread sections in the book, how the narrator even knew whose voice he was supposed to be using. I'm sure upon a reread it might become more obvious after having a sense of the cast of characters already on hand.<br /><br /><b>Why I waffled between 4 and 5 stars</b><br />Because I can already see myself going back to this book to read (or listen) to certain scenes, chapters, or even big chunks of the book someday. But, probably not the whole thing from start to finish. </span>
  <a data-text-id="792436856089559230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13864747538959300322">My first impulse was to just copy some old, obscure review of 'the Recognitions' and claim it as my own. Alas, even the reviewers, academics, and cult worshipers of the God of PoMo all seem at once thunderstruck AND intimidated by Gaddis' opus. <br/><br/>What I understood was brilliant, what I didn't understand is most likely obscene. This is not a novel for the casual beach read, although as I write this, I am on a beach...washing sand out of my ebbs and salt off my flow, so never mind.</span>

<div id="review_873608286" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/873608286" />
    <a title="Lee Foust" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust"><img alt="Lee Foust" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875445p2/13984676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/873608286?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781564786913" href="/book/show/11786836-the-recognitions">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13984676-lee-foust?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer873608286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11130300881523821797">Even the great William Gass in his introduction to <i>The Recognitions</i> has to digress and to hem and haw about reductionism and basically admit that one can’t really write about a novel such as this without diminishing its greatness rather than revealing more of it. Then he takes the ultimate cop-out—to me who thinks archetypal readings are the deadest of all dead ends—by noting the novel’s archetypal structure, which, frankly, I didn’t see at all. <i>The Recognitions</i> is too big, with too many charact</span>
  <span id="freeText11130300881523821797" style="display:none">Even the great William Gass in his introduction to <i>The Recognitions</i> has to digress and to hem and haw about reductionism and basically admit that one can’t really write about a novel such as this without diminishing its greatness rather than revealing more of it. Then he takes the ultimate cop-out—to me who thinks archetypal readings are the deadest of all dead ends—by noting the novel’s archetypal structure, which, frankly, I didn’t see at all. <i>The Recognitions</i> is too big, with too many characters, too many surprises and co-incidences, to come close to resembling anything else that I have ever read or story I’ve heard told—and this is surely a part of its greatness—it’s an archetype (read unoriginality) destroyer.<br /><br /><i>The Recognitions</i> is unique and unique in ways that are themselves unique. My God, it’s genius at work—probably the greatest American novel and the greatest American novel in the way that Babe Ruth was the greatest home-run hitter the year he first slugged fifty, hitting fifty four, when his closest rival, Sisler, hit a mere nineteen. Yes, <i>The Recognitions</i> is that good. Amazing in its use of language: inventive musical phrasing that renews American English in every sentence for nearly 1000 pages. Finishing it (stuck on an airplane for twelve and a half hours) I passed on to a perfectly good, even celebrated, contemporary novel—competent, quite well written by contemporary standards actually, and every sentence of this really good new novel sounded like “See Dick run,” in the wake of Gaddis’ exceptional prose. Ouch.<br /><br /><i>The Recognitions</i> is the kind of novel that makes a writer despair because you simply cannot compete. Still, it’s also a bar to shoot for, an ideal to inspire perhaps, a joy to know that such things are possible, however humbling.<br /><br />Of course, critically, you have to begin with the comparison with Joyce and Ulysses. By choosing what Joyce called “the continental method” of denoting dialog, an em-dash at the beginning of a spoken passage rather than quotation marks at both ends, the novel itself invites the comparison. Also the musicality of the language, and even perhaps a bit of the cynicism draws me back to Joyce, even despite the self-proclaimed simple “love one another” theme of the Irish giant’s most celebrated novel.<br /><br />First and foremost I think I found <i>The Recognitions</i> to be the greatest of all cynically corrosive narratives. And while cynicism is troubling—how, under the cloud of its nihilism will we ever again do anything good or worthwhile to better ourselves, society, humanity??—it’s also the greatest lighter fluid, cleanser of human folly and false, failed ideologies—what F. T. Marinetti really meant, I think, or what he should have meant anyway, when he spoke of Futurist “Hygiene.” The cynicism of <i>The Recognitions</i>’ narrative is not unlike the irony of Dante’s <i>Inferno</i>, in which every societal value is presented more or less convincingly by the damned sinners in the form of a series of lame excuses for their damnation. The irony of their situation—damned for eternity by an infallible moral arbiter—makes their earnest self-justifications ludicrous and transparent. Such denuding of human folly in light of artistic and religious absolutism lies at the heart of the <i>The Recognitions</i>’ long story and importantly intertwining characters’ many follies and struggles.<br /><br />And what is a recognition if not exactly that: seeing, becoming aware, seeing-again the folly of one’s own actions? This act of sudden self-awareness, an epiphany (to Joyce) or satori (to Kerouac), an act repeated over and over again by the characters in the novel, is also more than comparable to the epiphanies of Joyce’s <i>Dubliners</i>, a text whose cynicism is filled with tear-jerking pathos—a book that could only have been written from the perspective of an Irishman in Trieste. (Gaddis’ characters go abroad themselves in order to create and swim in their own alienation and lostness. The protagonist’s last scene involves his being told to move on by a Foreign Legionnaire—the eternal foreigner will always know only himself, having no context upon which to rely to defer his actions of choice.) Self-revelation and recognition drive all of the characters in <i>The Recognitions</i> mad in one way or another, cause more than one suicide attempt, produce a shitload of partying, drinking, drug-taking, art making, and writing (of both the real and the forged) in a kind of desperate self-defensive orgy to stave off real self-examination—albeit noting how ironically close to self-revelation all of these activities actually are/become.<br /><br />The novel, therefore, revolves around the need for self-examination in order to achieve authenticity and its conflict with the terror of applying perspective and context to one’s self-involvement, creating a cynicism of contextual perspective that can only lead to feelings of worthlessness in an individual in the face of history, of institutions, and the collective nature of our species. Egotism is all we have, really, to combat meaninglessness and yet egotism is the most meaningless gesture of all in light of our biology, of physics, and, of course, time’s utter eventual annihilation of all human endeavor. I think, therefore, that displaying so brilliantly and forcefully and repetitively (in all of its many particulars) this ongoing conflict of meaning/significance and how it effects our perspective (how we recognize the self and other) is the greatest narrative statement upon the human condition (first world mid-20th century and bourgeois as it is—but, well, that’s the world I inherited so it speaks to me) that I have ever read. <i>The Recognitions</i> makes Camus’s <i>L'etranger</i> look like a too-pat-to-be-worth-my-time O. Henry moralistic fable by comparison. Just amazing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11130300881523821797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_873608286'><a id="like_count_review_873608286" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/873608286?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">23 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F395058.The_Recognitions">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_707241942" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/707241942" />
    <a title="Ploppy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9950790-ploppy"><img alt="Ploppy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527152864p2/9950790.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/707241942?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ploppy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ploppy" href="/user/show/9950790-ploppy">Ploppy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781564786913" href="/book/show/11786836-the-recognitions">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9950790-ploppy?shelf=fatties">fatties</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer707241942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15916216333100067926">It’s always rather worrying when somebody asks you what the book you’re reading is about, and you are at a loss for an answer. “Well... there’s this guy called Wyatt... er... Gwin? Gwy-yon?” (you suddenly realize you don’t really know how to pronounce his surname) “and he forges paintings and is rather strange and...” And you give up. Not to say <i>The Recognitions</i> is plotless, but it definitely doesn’t have a clear direction and is a dense, highly ambitious and probably voluntarily confusing work.</span>
  <span id="freeText15916216333100067926" style="display:none">It’s always rather worrying when somebody asks you what the book you’re reading is about, and you are at a loss for an answer. “Well... there’s this guy called Wyatt... er... Gwin? Gwy-yon?” (you suddenly realize you don’t really know how to pronounce his surname) “and he forges paintings and is rather strange and...” And you give up. Not to say <i>The Recognitions</i> is plotless, but it definitely doesn’t have a clear direction and is a dense, highly ambitious and probably voluntarily confusing work.<br /><br />The opening chapter is one of the most extraordinary pieces of literature I have ever read. Combining lyrical beauty with a moving study of a child gradually alienating himself from other people whilst learning to paint all by himself, seemingly forgotten by his father, over-shadowed by a bigoted aunt and with only a photograph of his dead mother to remember her. All of this sprinkled with short jabs of satiric humour. I thought to myself: if this holds up for 900 pages this could well become my favourite book. Since you probably saw the three stars I gave it, you can deduce that this was not the case.<br /><br />William Gaddis is undoubtedly a talented writer. There are long polyphonic dialogues that never lose the reader, and an almost surreal humour at times. The way faking paintings becomes a mirror of the hypocrisy of social interactions is an interesting and original theme, you have to admire the research Gaddis went into in order to make the faking process believable.<br /><br />However, he is not always as funny as he thinks. At one point, a movie mogul brandishes his cheque book explaining to one the characters that it once stopped a bullet, thus saving him. It made me laugh, but it also points to the sometimes glaring lack of subtlety and heavy symbolism that pervades the novel (money is the new religion!!! :o :o :o ). This is especially true of the secondary characters, who are more or less allegories of societies' various faults, rather than actual characters. This enables the author to frankly indulge in some very obvious satire, with characters saying stupid things, often trying to pass off as cultured by misquoting something, giving the reader the very uncomfortable feeling of being superior to these characters, whereas in the novel’s best moments (which in my opinion are too rare), it is the reader himself who feels criticized.<br /><br />Some of the main characters are more interesting. Otto especially is a hilarious portrait of a would-be artist and in general his plotline is a great 101 course on what not to do if you want to be a writer. Apparently some of the ridiculous things he does (pretending to have witnessed a revolution in Central America and on his return walking around with an unbroken arm in a sling), are things Gaddis did. Otto’s father, although not often there, is a wonderfully touching, comically tragic character, and also has parallels with Gaddis' father. This doesn't stop Gaddis from being cruel to both of them. Wyatt Gwyon is intriguing at first but is so undecipherable it becomes tiresome. Stanley, a composer working on a mass, seems rather contrived among the other would-be artist figures. Esme come across as extremely irritating. She plays the typical Ever-Elusive Mysterious Female, full of “disarming” comments, pursued by each artist character because he persuades himself he needs her as his muse (her name is even sort of an anagram of muse, isn't it? isn't it?) or something (except Wyatt coz he's so cool he's the one eluding her). Clichéd and groan-inducing. The plot itself starts off rather focused but goes all Game of Thrones towards the end: characters jump out of windows, stab each other and emasculate themselves, or else go mad if they aren't already mad. Seems like an easy way to end plotlines to me.<br /><br />Regarding the style, another reviewer pointed out that a character in a George Eliot novel always writes “commenced” instead of “began”. Well, Gaddis does the same thing and it feels pretentious and unnecessary. Much like the tidbits of French (which luckily I understand) and Hungarian (which unfortunately I don't) which seem thrown in to impress the reader more than anything else. I would like to point out that I have nothing against demanding books. I thoroughly enjoyed long and complex works such as <i>Ulysses</i> and <i>Gravity's Rainbow</i>, works that <i>The Recognitions</i> is often compared to (which is why I came to read it), however I always felt that their difficulty and erudition were necessary, whereas here it sometimes (not always) felt superficially tacked on, much like what the characters it is heavy-handedly mocking do.<br /><br />This brings me to another gripe: <i>The Recognitions</i>, to me, is a work of bitterness. There is nothing wrong with being bitter <i>per se</i> if you can communicate it in an entertaining way and make the reader feel that this bitterness is justified, giving the reader a heightened sense of lucidity. Many great satiric works are ferociously, wickedly, viciously funny, such as <i>Catch-22</i>. Yet with Heller, there is a certain warmth and, more importantly, a sense of necessity in his condemnation of warfare and how it is organized. Gaddis' attacks feel often misguided, evil-minded, and so caricatural that they come off as artificial (all atheists are science worshippers), stemming more from Gaddis' personal problems than from reality.<br /><br />To me, <i>The Recognitions</i>, as much as I wanted to like it (especially after that glorious first chapter) is not the scandalously overlooked masterpiece it is hailed as, but an ambitious work by a gifted author who paradoxically seems already full of bitterness from not having had the recognition (no pun intended) he might have deserved, before having actually published the book. I might get back to Gaddis some other time, because <i>The Recognitions</i> is far from being an uninteresting book, but not right now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15916216333100067926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1833013658" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1833013658" />
    <a title="Christopher Robinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62328457-christopher-robinson"><img alt="Christopher Robinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503277082p2/62328457.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1833013658?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher Robinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher Robinson" href="/user/show/62328457-christopher-robinson">Christopher Robinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781564786913" href="/book/show/11786836-the-recognitions">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62328457-christopher-robinson?shelf=very-favorite-novels">very-favorite-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62328457-christopher-robinson?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62328457-christopher-robinson?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62328457-christopher-robinson?shelf=read-and-kept">read-and-kept</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1833013658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4224052846963222611">I can’t add anything with my review that hasn’t already been said better elsewhere by much sharper readers. But reading The Recognitions was life-changing. One of the more incredible reading experiences of my life thus far. Here are an assortment of reasons why.<br/><br/>- THE HUMOR. This book doesn’t get enough credit for being funny, and Gaddis doesn’t get enough credit for writing brilliant comedy, but Gaddis wrote BRILLIANT comedy and The Recognitions is no exception. It’s also dramatic, deeply sad, c</span>
  <span id="freeText4224052846963222611" style="display:none">I can’t add anything with my review that hasn’t already been said better elsewhere by much sharper readers. But reading The Recognitions was life-changing. One of the more incredible reading experiences of my life thus far. Here are an assortment of reasons why.<br /><br />- THE HUMOR. This book doesn’t get enough credit for being funny, and Gaddis doesn’t get enough credit for writing brilliant comedy, but Gaddis wrote BRILLIANT comedy and The Recognitions is no exception. It’s also dramatic, deeply sad, cynical, pessimistic, wordy and dense, but there’s A LOT of humor to reward you for the occasionally quite challenging reading experience. I laughed out loud so often while reading that I lost count, and I can say that about all of Gaddis’s novels, really. But even here, in his first novel, his humor was readily apparent.<br /><br />- THE DIALOGUE. Those party scenes where everyone is talking over each other, drunkenly milling about and careening from conversation to conversation are some of the greatest feats of dialogue writing I’ve ever encountered. Gaddis is the master of realistic dialogue, and his second novel JR (quite understandably) gets most of the praise on this front, but the dialogue here in The Recognitions is of a similarly high caliber (as is the dialogue in his other novels, Carpenter’s Gothic and A Frolic of His Own).<br /><br />- OTTO. Otto is one of the funniest (and ultimately pathetic) characters in all of American literature. Constantly pursuing literary greatness but seemingly unwilling to do much actual writing, faking an arm injury and wearing a sling in order to have a conversation piece, every scene with Otto was pure gold, and often had me laughing aloud.<br /><br />- STANLEY. I loved the Stanley character as well. A deeply religious composer, terrified (ironically, given his eventual fate, particularly of buildings collapsing while he is inside of them), guilt-ridden, sexually repressed and striving for musical greatness while ACTUALLY putting in the work, Stanley makes a great counterpoint to Otto’s constant bullshitting and half-assery. Although ultimately, his ambition and obsession get the better of him and he is ultimately killed in the process of performing his magnum opus on an organ in a centuries old cathedral when said cathedral collapses on him as a result of him playing it too loudly. What a character, that Stanley. Unforgettable.<br /><br />- WYATT’S STORY ARC. Leaves the priesthood, disappointing his father in the process, for a career in painting, which he excels at. Moves to NYC to launch his art career, but is undermined by unscrupulous reviewers and lives the life of a starving artist until the odious Recktall Brown comes around and offers him a Faustian bargain: paint new works in the style of the Dutch/Flemish masters, which Brown will sell as newly discovered original works to unsuspecting wealthy buyers. Naturally, his time spent on the forgeries gradually takes up more and more of the time he ought to be spend on making his own art, and at this point things start to go quite badly for poor Wyatt. Most notably, he undergoes a massive identity crisis. What follows is one of the greatest stories ever told, not to mention perhaps the greatest satire of the art world written during the 20th century. <br /><br />And lastly, but probably most importantly:<br /><br />- HOW SHOCKINGLY READABLE IT ACTUALLY WAS! Everyone told me this would be the most difficult read ever, basically. And in some ways, they weren’t wrong to caution me: the writing is indeed dense; the page count massive; the characters talk a lot about art that I had superficial knowledge of (at best) before reading; there’s a lot of religion/theology and a good amount of untranslated non-English (French, Hungarian, German, etc.), not to mention technical musical language galore. But even so, it wasn’t particularly difficult to follow and it wasn’t a chore to read. You don’t need to get every little reference or allusion to appreciate this novel. If you do, it will undoubtedly enrich the experienced, but in no way is it absolutely necessary. Seriously, it’s challenging but not truly difficult. It actually tells a story (LOTS of them), has a plot, has engaging characters that do interesting things. It has all the elements of an eminently readable novel, it just has them in abundance to the point where reading it seems a daunting undertaking. But it really isn’t, or at the very least isn’t as difficult as most would have you believe. <br /><br />Anyway, read this novel. And then read all of Gaddis. Then tell other people to read Gaddis. And then reread him. He was a one of a kind writer, and The Recognitions is truly a one of a kind novel. Masterpiece.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4224052846963222611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14244646853724244011"> <b>We live in Rome, he says, turning his face to the room again,<br/>-Caligula's Rome, with a new circus of vulgar bestialized suffering in the newspapers every morning. The masses, the fetid masses, he says, bringing all his weight to his feet.-How can they even suspect a self who can do more, when they live under absolutely no obligation. There are so few beautiful things in the world.</b><br/><br/>Such higher machinations proved beyond me. So much was required. Too often I was found wanting. The Recognitions is a</span>
  <span id="freeText14244646853724244011" style="display:none"> <b>We live in Rome, he says, turning his face to the room again,<br />-Caligula's Rome, with a new circus of vulgar bestialized suffering in the newspapers every morning. The masses, the fetid masses, he says, bringing all his weight to his feet.-How can they even suspect a self who can do more, when they live under absolutely no obligation. There are so few beautiful things in the world.</b><br /><br />Such higher machinations proved beyond me. So much was required. Too often I was found wanting. The Recognitions is an uncharted continent. My cap is tipped to those unlacing the Incognito. I made my way through it but am left baffled, perhaps awaiting some Stanley whom will search for me and yield elucidation.<br /><br />Color me knackered. I am weary and dehydrated and manipulating my favorite images from The Recognitions like some layman chancing upon talismans. It is unjust to compare, though I transgress: I liked Ulysses far more, found such poetic, whereas -- despite the marvel Gaddis engenders -- I feel dirty and bleak. There is a thread of thought which finds that conclusion one of design. <br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_273693251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273693251" />
    <a title="Christopher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5745485-christopher"><img alt="Christopher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518126470p2/5745485.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273693251?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher" href="/user/show/5745485-christopher">Christopher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=500-pages-or-more">500-pages-or-more</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=postmodern">postmodern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=nameless-characters">nameless-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5745485-christopher?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273693251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14918063850823380345">First things first: let's get the size out of the way. Yes, it is a long book, 956 pages plus a fifteen page introduction. It takes a long time to read, but really it's only the size of three averagely sized novels put together. Its heft will not strain your pectoralis major nor cause any other physical distress... it's a book, not an exercise regimen, so there is no further need to talk about its physical enormity or the pain it simply cannot inflict upon your corporeal self.<br/><br/>The noncorporeal he</span>
  <span id="freeText14918063850823380345" style="display:none">First things first: let's get the size out of the way. Yes, it is a long book, 956 pages plus a fifteen page introduction. It takes a long time to read, but really it's only the size of three averagely sized novels put together. Its heft will not strain your pectoralis major nor cause any other physical distress... it's a book, not an exercise regimen, so there is no further need to talk about its physical enormity or the pain it simply cannot inflict upon your corporeal self.<br /><br />The noncorporeal heft of this novel, however, is a different thing indeed. Its pages are packed with an <i>erudition</i> (italicized for its unavoidable usage in connection with Gaddis's work) and thematic weight that give the narrative a wonderful and tremendous gravity. It's no beach read in the normally accepted sense of the term (although I did read parts of it on the beach and found that it's not an impossible task to do so.) Much like a work to which I will later compare this one (and one of which the reader is surely aware), The Recognitions deals with terrible and heartbreaking circumstances of both lovable and hatable characters. Its prose is often dense and rife with classical allusions. (See below for a list of works fit for a study of Gaddis's influences.)<br /><br />All such erudition, density, and weighty themes should not give you, dear reader, pause when considering embarkation upon its reading. It's full of delights; let me number them for you.<br /><br />One, wonderful prose. It is chokkefull of beautifully or masterfully written sentences. In searching for an apt example, I need to look no further than its first paragraph, which I present to you in full:<blockquote>Even Camilla had enjoyed masquerades, of the safe sort where the mask may be dropped at that critical moment it presumes itself as reality. But the procession up the foreign hill, bounded by cypress trees, impelled by the monotone chanting of the priest and retarded by hesitations at the fourteen stations of the Cross (not to speak of the funeral carriage in which she was riding, a white horse-drawn vehicle which resembled a baroque confectionery stand), might have ruffled the shy countenance of her soul, if it had been discernible.</blockquote> I don't suppose I need to explain to the reader why this is a wonderful paragraph, but I should explain my reasons for its inclusion here. It immediately announces the primary theme of the novel (or rather, not announces, but slyly slips it in there): that of fakery, forgery, counterfeiting, acting versus reality, et cetera. The mechanics of the sentences themselves create something complex yet highly readable. It invokes rich images that dance around the readers mind. (The image of a funeral carriage resembling a baroque confectionery stand is one that has stuck with me.)<br /><br />Two, an intriguing plot. It's hard to sum up such a novel, with its large cast of characters and diverse narrative arcs, but there are two characters whose stories steal the show. The first is Wyatt, whose mother Camilla, as we learned in the above excerpt, enjoyed masquerades. Wyatt, having lost his mother at a young age and grown up under the tutelage of a puritanical aunt and a rather erudite (there's that word again) but uninvolved and perhaps mad father, possesses the talent of a great artistic craftsman, able to copy and mimic the masters, but unable to create a work of his own. He thus commences a Faustian career of forgery. The second most notable character is Otto, a playwright who stands in contrast to Wyatt. He possesses little talent but great ambition and is utterly envious of Wyatt's abilities. He's altogether likable, however, and provides levity to the narrative as counterpoint to Wyatt's ultra-seriousness and navel-gazing.<br /><br />Three, there are just so many wonderful and unique details. There's a character who, when at parties, inscribes every book he can lay his hands upon, as if it was his work of art. There are callbacks, such as the use of lavender in the production of Wyatt's paint; a hundred pages before, the scent of lavender clung to Wyatt's model, though its source was unknown. Characters of apparent cameo-only importance later appear in a more significant role. (This is as good a place as any to recommend the keeping of a list of characters and brief descriptions so that you don't miss any of these reappearances or forget who someone is. I have included my <i>dramatis personae</i> for my personal records below; there may be some minor spoilers, so beware.)<br /><br />Four, it's funny. For instance, a writer complaining about a magazine's poor editing mangling his reputation: "Everybody knows I wrote that Rilke's references were occasionally <i>obscure</i>, and that dumb Radcliffe girl I had typed <i>obscene</i> when she copied it. I'd like to know who the hell copy-read that. And putting a <i>t</i> in genial..."<br /><br />Five, the names. Recktall Brown. Frank Sinisterra. Maude Munk. Adeline Thing. Mister Feddle. Charles Dickens. (Yes, Charles Dickens.) Big Anna the Swede. <br /><br />Six, the book is all so very <i>chavenet</i>.<br /><br />Seven, that's enough, isn't it?<br /><br />Now it's time to reveal the specter that's been hanging over this review, the previously referenced novel which cannot be avoided if we're talking about Gaddis's influence upon literature. The Recognitions may have been the single greatest influence upon David Foster Wallace's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182280182" rel="nofollow noopener">Infinite Jest</a>. They are similar in scope, with their huge casts of characters, multiple plotlines, and obsessive investigation of themes. Gaddis attacks the pervasiveness in American culture of counterfeiting and unoriginality the same way that Wallace attacks the ubiquity of entertainment. Each take a very dim view of the world. They are saturated with depression, suicide, addiction, and madness. The difference is that while Gaddis is resigned to his cynicism, Wallace tries to find a way to live in a broken world. Infinite Jest comes off almost saccharinely optimistic in comparison with The Recognitions. (Upon completion of both novels, the reader will have to decide which she prefers; are you a hopeless cynic or a hopeful optimist?) In reading Gaddis's book, it is almost impossible to avoid recalling Wallace's on a nearly page by page basis. Wallace's style of dialogue is lifted straight from this book, even down to the ellipses used to indicate a character's silence. <br /><br />(The influence of this book should not be underestimated. I know David Foster Wallace pretty well, so I feel qualified to talk about him. But other authors evidently inspired by Gaddis that I do not feel qualified to talk about, though deserve a mention nonetheless, include: Thomas Pynchon, Joseph McElroy, Don Delillo, William Gass, among authors; namely, postmodern writers.)<br /><br />So this is a towering achievement, an incredible development of style, and a huge influence on works to come. But what I find is so often lost in talking about giants of literature like this is that they are so <i>fun</i>. Really, this book is a pleasure to read. Yes, it is often demanding and requires rapt and unfailing attention, but it contains incredible pleasure. Do yourself a favor and read this daunting, difficult, wonderful, pleasing book.<br /><br /><br /><br /><u>A List of Works Fit for a Study of Gaddis's Influences</u><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7549908.The_Recognitions_of_Clement" title="The Recognitions of Clement by Douglas Hatten" rel="nofollow noopener">The Recognitions of Clement</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/408862.The_Golden_Bough" title="The Golden Bough by James George Frazer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Golden Bough</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1211478.James_George_Frazer" title="James George Frazer" rel="nofollow noopener">James George Frazer</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7588.A_Portrait_of_the_Artist_as_a_Young_Man" title="A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5144.James_Joyce" title="James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">James Joyce</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/406373.Faust" title="Faust by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Faust</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/285217.Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a><br />...among many others, though these are the most important. I'd welcome any obvious suggestions from others.<br /><br /><br /><u>Pertinent Links</u><br /><a href="http://www.theparisreview.org/interviews/2577/the-art-of-fiction-no-101-william-gaddis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Paris Review Gaddis Interview</a> (MUST READ!)<br /><a href="http://www.williamgaddis.org/recognitions/preface.shtml" rel="nofollow noopener">A Reader's Guide to William Gaddis's The Recognitions</a> (very helpful summaries, although beware minor spoilers for forthcoming chapters)<br /><a href="http://gaddis-drinking-club.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gaddis Drinking Club</a> (a blog which did a group read in 2005)<br /><a href="http://adilegian.com/FranzenGaddis.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">Mr. Difficult: William Gaddis and the Problem of Hard-to-Read Books</a> (by Jonathan Franzen; I didn't read it all)<br /><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=e3Czd7GwNy4" rel="nofollow noopener">William Gaddis in Conversation</a> (a poor quality video of Gaddis talking and smoking)<br /><br /><br /><i>Dramatis Personae</i><br />(hidden beneath a spoiler tag because the character descriptions contain minor spoilers; however, I'd suggest you at least open it up to see how long it is)<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[1. Camilla - died on a ship to Spain from appendicitis, buried in Spain<br />2. Reverend Gwyon - her husband, his family gets their money from an ancestor's oatmeal business<br />3. Wyatt - their son, the protagonist, becomes a painter and then a bridge draftsman, then gets involved in a counterfeiting ring<br />4. Esther - Wyatt's wife, has an affair with Otto and then Ellery, gets pregnant but loses the baby<br />5. Aunt May - Gwyon's father's sister, anti-Catholic, raised Camilla<br />6. Mary - the "silent cousin" who helped raise Camilla<br />7. Camilla's father - the Town Carpenter, has Indian blood, bad father<br />8.F rank Sinisterra - a conman, the ship's "surgeon" responsible for Camilla's death, his son is a friend of Esme, spent eight years in prison making steel plates for counterfeit twenty dollar bills<br />9. Fr. Manomuerta - a monk at the Estremadura friary, where Gwyon stays after burying Camilla; he is more accepting of Gwyon's Protestant-ness than the other monks<br />10. Miss Ardythe - organist, died at Camilla's and Gwyon's wedding<br />11. Señor Hermoso - chats with Gwyon about how San Zwingli needs a patron saint of its own<br />12.Janet - a girl with a tic, caught fornicating with the janitor behind the organ, taken in by Gwyon as a kitchen servant<br />13. Herr Koppel - Wyatt's art professor in Munich<br />Cremer - art critic in Paris, offers to write a good review of Wyatt's art in exchange for 10% of his sales<br />14. Han - Wyatt's friend from Munich<br />15. Don Bildow - Esther's friend, edits a magazine<br />16. Mrs. Bildow - his wife, invites Esther and Wyatt to a New Year's party<br />17. Anselm (real name is Arthur) - friend of the Bildows', watches the Bildows' daughter, alcoholic, calls Esme a whore at The Village party, pretends to be a doctor in a hospital so he can have sex with patients<br />18. Esteban - waiter at the Spanish restaurant Wyatt and Esther go to on New Year's Eve<br />19. John - Wyatt's friend from priest school, who ran into Wyatt and Esther on New Year's Eve and Esther didn't even know Wyatt had ever studied to become a priest, runs into Wyatt two years later, had seen Wyatt's father <br />20. Otto - Esther's friend, talks with her about Wyatt, is writing a play in which he is named Gordon, has affair with Esther, goes to a banana plantation, when he comes back he pretends to have been injured in a revolution<br />21. Recktall Brown - hired Wyatt to do some restoration work, talked to him about why he never does originals, editor of "In Dreams I Kiss Your Hand", an athology dedicated to Esme<br />22. Benny - Wyatt's supervisor at the bridge designers, signs his name on Wyatt's work, called looking for Wyatt after he stopped coming to work, was at Esther's Christmas Eve party<br />23. Rose - Esther's sister, Esther dreamed about flying kites with her, she was at a mental hospital<br />24. Ellery - an adman, replaces Otto as Esther's lover<br />25. Jesse Franks - Otto's friend at the banana plantation in Central America, tough guy<br />26. Maude Munk - in an insurance dispute after a taxi crash, trying to adopt a baby<br />27. Arny Munk - her husband, introduced to Maude by their analyst<br />28. Herschel - Maude's handsome gay friend, says that Wyatt is involved in a counterfeiting ring and that Esther is pregnant, ghostwriter for politicians, punches Hannah at the Village party<br />29. Adeline Thing - Herschel's date, is helping Fuller to work black magic on Recktall Brown with the aid of her dead daughter Elsie<br />30. Agnes Deigh - literary agent, just got back from Puerto Rico, was at the party, lapsed Catholic, turns down publishing Otto's play, gets a letter saying her brother died in combat, witnesses a dentist beating a woman through her office window<br />31. Harry Deigh - her husband, a published writer<br />32. Hannah - dumb girl at the Village art party, gets punched by Herschel for telling him he doesn't even know who he is<br />33. Charles Dickens (Charley) - suicidal ex-army man escorted from the party by Herschel, has a silver plate in his skull and scars on his throat and wrists, feels guilty for being in a plane that dropped an atomic bomb, was taken to Bellevue (reported by Stanley)<br />34. Max - artist of the painting at the Village party<br />35. Stanley - musician at Village party, trying to write a mass for his mother who has diabetes and is dying<br />36. Ed Feasley - went to Harvard with Otto, reckless prankster, called Max in Otto's play, stole a human leg from the morgue with Otto<br />37. Mister Feddle - an old poet at the Village party, pays to have his work published, inscribes random books at parties, wears an alarm clock around his neck<br />38. Esme - Otto's crush at the Village party, a painter's model, poet, manic depressive, heroin addict, gets called a whore by Anselm<br />39. Big Anna the Swede - flamboyant homosexual cross dresser<br />the critic - a writer whose work won't be published by Bildow's magazine because of the bad editing of his previously run article<br />40. Edna Mims - Otto's ex-girlfriend, works on a magazine, now dating Max, works for Recktall Brown, messed up the critic's article by bad copyediting<br />41. Chaby Sinisterra - friend of Esme, described as wearing all black with a black mustache, son of Frank Sinisterra who was responsible for Camilla's death, injured in the war, addicted to morphine<br />42. Fuller - Recktall Brown's African-American assistant, bought a ticket to abandon Brown but was too stupid to keep it a secret<br />43. Basil Valentine - friend of Recktall Brown, confidant of Wyatt, a spy for the Roman church and a Hungarian regime<br />44. Dalner - an art inspector, sometimes questions the authenticity of works to drive the price down and buy it<br />45. Mr. Pivner - Otto's father, diabetic, Otto has never met him, obsessed with Carnegie's How to Win Friends and Influence People<br />46. Eddie Zefnic - office boy in Pivner's office building<br />47. Jerry - Pivner's newspaper guy<br />48. Popeye - woman crossdresser from the drag party, was in trouble for kidnapping a seal "for immoral purposes", no one knows her real name<br />49. Rudy - male crossdresser from the drag party, tricked Ed Feasly into thinking he was a woman, is designing sports costumes for nuns<br />50. Cleopatra - male dressed as Cleopatra at the drag party, hit on Otto<br />51. Victoria Hall - Big Anna's friend, wrote a book<br />52. Albert Hall - her husband, wrote a play<br />53. Martin - talked to Basil Valentine about Clement's Recognitions<br />54. Mr. Crotcher - was at Esther's party, wrote a novel about ant life which he has translated into 19 languages, has never been published<br />55. Buster - was at Esther's party, said Crotcher's book must be "awfully dirty"<br />56. James Leak - at Esther's party, published a book called With Gun and Camera in Flatbush and Greenpoint<br />57. Arthur - bearded guest at Esther's party, writing book of a new life of Christ<br />58. Minna Vesendorf (Izarra) - talked with Arthur<br />59. Mr. Inononu - a Hungarian secret agent on a mission to assassinate a Rumanian scholar of ancient Egypt<br />60. Yak - a Rumanian scholar of ancient Egypt, Inononu's target<br />61. Mr. Schmuck (of Twentieth Century Schmuck), 62. Mr. Sonnenschein, 63. M. Cremer, 64. Miss Stein (with Mr. Sonnenschein) - attendees at Recktall Brown's Christmas Eve party<br />65. Reverend "Dick"/Richard - Reverend Gwyon's replacement<br />66. Marga - the prostitute in Madrid hired by Wyatt<br />67. Pastora - Wyatt's second girlfriend in Spain, has his child<br />68-?. I petered out keeping track of characters near the end. There are a few more that should be here. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_549689464" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/549689464" />
    <a title="Declan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5848260-declan"><img alt="Declan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427664982p2/5848260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/549689464?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Declan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Declan" href="/user/show/5848260-declan">Declan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781843541660" href="/book/show/28435.The_Recognitions">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5848260-declan?shelf=dalkey-archive">dalkey-archive</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer549689464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1330152965183054402">Given that The Recognitions ends on Easter Sunday, this would seem to be the perfect day to review a book which is suffused with references to the bible and to manifestations of religious impulses, delusions and repudiations. Somewhere in the rites and wrongs; the tribulations and transubstantiations there is a story about the impossibility of being who we want to be; a profound and, however bizarrely presented, convincing examination of the ontological turmoil which is our lot. <br/><br/>More important t</span>
  <span id="freeText1330152965183054402" style="display:none">Given that The Recognitions ends on Easter Sunday, this would seem to be the perfect day to review a book which is suffused with references to the bible and to manifestations of religious impulses, delusions and repudiations. Somewhere in the rites and wrongs; the tribulations and transubstantiations there is a story about the impossibility of being who we want to be; a profound and, however bizarrely presented, convincing examination of the ontological turmoil which is our lot. <br /><br />More important than the questions raised, on a first reading in any case, is the form the novel takes. Teasing is central to the Gaddis style, both in the sense of teasing out meaning from abstruse references and in the sense of teasing the reader. He pushes us to try harder than we would have to do with a conventional book, but by God, and by Gaddis, it is worth it. As in life, that which is hard-won is appreciated far more than that which is merely given. Nothing in The Recognitions is a given. Almost no detail is delivered without ambiguous implications or an allusion to the shadowing forces of myth, religion or art. Indeed ,you could almost say that, in this 956 page book, nothing is ever said directly; all is implied. Dislocation recurs throughout the novel and doubt is constant: the search for authenticity is central to every action:<br /><br />"-and so when you're working, it's your own work, Basil Valentine said. - And when you attach the signature?. . .-Yes when I attach the signature, he said dropping his head again, - that changes everything, when I attach the signature and. . . loose it."<br /><br />Or, later in the book:<br /><br />"The love I have from others is not love of me, but where they try to find themselves, loving me. I dream and I wake up, and then at that moment you are somewhere being real to other people; and they are part of your reality; and I am not. . .But you are the only person I am real with. . ."<br /><br /><br />That desperate search for authenticity is also often demonstrated by very funny displays of inauthenticity:<br /><br />"Once in my convent school, I remember when we were all sent to look at a reliquary. It was. . .I don't know, a splinter of the cross, or a crumb of something. They even had one they said contained a bit of the original darkness that Moses called down on the world, imagine."<br /><br /><br />There is especially sharp humour in the way Gaddis portrays the malleability of his characters presentation of themselves at several large gatherings; terrific set-pieces replete with misunderstanding, pretensions, arguments and people straining beyond their limits to succeed in impressing those they are speaking to. I especially enjoyed the deliberate undermining of these occasions by one set of characters who, before going out, decided on their "latest arcanum", a made up word which they would use in several contexts:<br /><br />"Chavenet. It really doesn't mean anything, but it's familiar to everyone if you say it quickly. They mention a painter's style, you nod and say, Rather. . .chavenet, or, He's rather derivative of, Chavenet, wouldn't you say? Spending the summer? Yes, in the south of France, a little villa near Chavenet."<br /><br />A multitude of voices endeavour to be heard above the background drone of other voices, radio ads and music (the soundtrack to this book would be a superb selection of early, baroque and classical music, with some folk and popular songs along the way). Amidst them all, and central to the novel, is Wyatt, painter, forger, never-to-be preacher - almost nowhere called by his name - and unable to form a complete sentence or a coherent, communicable thought. Lost, as all these characters eventually are in - if I may dare to emulate Gaddis for a moment - Fuller's "black dismal dungeon of despair" (that's William Fuller (1608-1675), not Fuller the character in the book who works as slave/servant for the indelicately named Recktall Brown!). It is, ultimately, a grim read, but an utterly engrossing and intricate read too. So much of what is possible in a novel is given real, expansive form here and while it may be necessary, sometimes, to read a paragraph twice to untangle its meaning, I always found a basic, underlying coherence in everything Gaddis wrote. Persistence was always rewarded and there is invaluable help here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.williamgaddis.org/">http://www.williamgaddis.org/</a>. How drab and unimaginative many books will seem after this. The Recognitions is chavenet, utterly chavenet. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1330152965183054402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mtDaq6QQXigl8CIX1FjHfiFwrWD65WTLEPibBLOTTClYho5HcthJLop4tNmnKs14gyW4t4zmdUiIrmwcy2G5Ww==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Kr/fICecq+p9dhUeZdI14jheCrLtk+pZz+1weK6txiro6YvM8VS87NL+g9AWoD/kmgsfZZuQ+9pXu4dg1l8zWA==')})
  }

Written in what DeLillo calls &quot;super-omniscience&quot; the sentences sweep from young Cotter Martin as he jumps the gate to the press box, soars over the radio waves, runs out to the diamond, slides in on a fast ball, pops into the stands where J. Edgar Hoover is sitting with a drunken Jackie Gleason and a splenetic Frank Sinatra, and learns of the Soviet Union\'s second detonation of a nuclear bomb. It\'s an absolutely thrilling literary moment. When Bobby Thomson hits Branca\'s pitch into the outstretched hand of Cotter—the &quot;shot heard around the world&quot;—and Jackie Gleason pukes on Sinatra\'s shoes, the events of the next few decades are set in motion, all threaded together by the baseball as it passes from hand to hand.\n\n&quot;It\'s all falling indelibly into the past,&quot; writes DeLillo, a past that he carefully recalls and reconstructs with acute grace. Jump from Giants Stadium to the Nevada desert in 1992, where Nick Shay, who now owns the baseball, reunites with the artist Kara Sax. They had been brief and unlikely lovers 40 years before, and it is largely through the events, spinoffs, and coincidental encounters of their pasts that DeLillo filters the Cold War experience. He believes that &quot;global events may alter how we live in the smallest ways,&quot; and as the book steps back in time to 1951, over the following 800-odd pages, we see just how those events alter lives. This reverse narrative allows the author to strip away the detritus of history and pop culture until we get to the story\'s pure elements: the bomb, the baseball, and the Bronx. In an epilogue as breathless and stunning as the prologue, DeLillo fast-forwards to a near future in which ruthless capitalism, the Internet, and a new, hushed faith have replaced the Cold War\'s blend of dread and euphoria.\n\nThrough fragments and interlaced stories—including those of highway killers, artists, celebrities, conspiracists, gangsters, nuns, and sundry others—DeLillo creates a fragile web of connected experience, a communal Zeitgeist that encompasses the messy whole of five decades of American life, wonderfully distilled.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15479255437449604310\"
